     \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs1\" name=\"apjab7ffbs1\">1.\u00a0Introduction</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Strong lensing systems (Walsh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib57\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib57\">1979</a>; Lynds &amp; Petrosian <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib34\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib34\">1986</a>; Paczynski <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib39\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib39\">1987</a>; Soucail et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib47\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib47\">1987</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib48\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib48\">1988</a>) have been used to study how dark matter is distributed in galaxies and clusters (e.g., Kochanek <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib28\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib28\">1991</a>; Koopmans &amp; Treu <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib29\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib29\">2002</a>; Bolton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib4\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib4\">2006</a>; Koopmans et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib30\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib30\">2006</a>; Vegetti &amp; Koopmans <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib56\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib56\">2009</a>; Tessore et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib52\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib52\">2016</a>). As a cosmological probe, time delays in multiply lensed quasars provide competitive constraints on the Hubble constant <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> (e.g., Refsdal <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib43\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib43\">1964</a>; Blandford &amp; Narayan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib2\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib2\">1992</a>; Suyu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib51\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib51\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib50\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib50\">2013</a>; Treu &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib54\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib54\">2016</a>; Bonvin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib5\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib5\">2017</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib61\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib61\">2019</a>) independent of the distance ladder approach.</p><p>In recent years, highly magnified, multiply imaged supernovae (SNe), both core-collapse (Kelly et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib27\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib27\">2015</a>) and Type Ia (Quimby et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib42\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib42\">2014</a>; Goobar et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib21\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib21\">2017</a>), have been discovered. With their well-characterized brightness time evolution in optical and near-infrared wavelengths (the SN lightcurves), such strongly lensed SNe are ideally suited to measure time delays and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub> in future surveys (e.g., Goldstein &amp; Nugent <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib18\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib18\">2017</a>; Goldstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib19\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib19\">2019</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib20\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib20\">2018</a>; Wojtak et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib59\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib59\">2019</a>) .</p><p>In this paper we show that hundreds of new strong lensing systems can be found in the three band imaging data (<em>grz</em>) from the Dark Energy Spectroscopic Instrument (DESI) Legacy Surveys<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn1\">12</a></sup>\n (Dey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib13\">2019</a>). To find these lenses from a data set that covers one third of the sky, we adopt the residual neural network (He et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib22\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib22\">2015a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib23\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib23\">2015b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib24\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib24\">2016</a>) developed by Lanusse et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">2018</a>), the winning algorithm of the Strong Gravitational Lens Finding Challenge (Metcalf et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib35\">2019</a>), to automate the process as much as possible.</p><p>This paper is organized as follows. A brief description of the Legacy Surveys is given in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs2\">2</a>. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs3\">3</a>, we describe our methodology and training sample. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs4\">4</a>, we show the inference results and present our best strong lensing system candidates. We discuss our results in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs5\">5</a>, and conclude in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs6\">6</a>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs2\" name=\"apjab7ffbs2\">2.\u00a0Observations</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The details of the DESI Legacy Imaging Surveys are described in Dey et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib13\">2019</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib13\">D19</a>). Here we present a brief summary. The Legacy Surveys consist of three projects: the Dark Energy Camera Legacy Survey (DECaLS), observed by the Dark Energy Camera (DECam; Flaugher et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib16\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib16\">2015</a>) on the 4 m Blanco telescope at the Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory; the Beijing-Arizona Sky Survey (BASS), by the 90Prime camera (Williams et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib58\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib58\">2004</a>) on the Bok 2.3 m telescope owned and operated by the University of Arizona located on Kitt Peak; and the Mayall <em>z</em>-band Legacy Survey (MzLS), by the Mosaic3 camera (Dey et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib12\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib12\">2016</a>) on the 4 m Mayall telescope at Kitt Peak National Observatory. Together they will ultimately cover ~14,000 deg<sup>2</sup> of the extragalactic sky visible from the northern hemisphere in <em>grz</em> bands, with a 5<em>\u03c3 z</em>-band median limiting magnitude of 22.5\u00a0mag for galaxies with an exponential disk profile with <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbieqn1.gif\" alt=\"${r}_{i,\\mathrm{half}}=0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 45$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{r}_{i,\\mathrm{half}}=0\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 45</script></span></span>.</p><p>The combined survey footprint is split into two contiguous areas by the Galactic plane. DECaLS covers the ~9000 deg<sup>2</sup> <em>\u03b4</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0+32\u00b0 sub-region of the Legacy Surveys. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a> shows the different regions in the the Legacy Surveys footprint and the depth of the <em>z</em>-band observation for DR7, which is indicative of the completeness of the survey.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0DESI Legacy Surveys footprint in an equal area Aitoff projection in equatorial coordinates with the DECaLS and MzLS/BASS regions highlighted with red and green outlines, respectively. Above the Galactic plane, there is a small amount of overlap between the surveys. For DECaLS, patches with different shades of blue indicate the depth in <em>z</em> band: light blue for between three and seven passes; medium blue, between seven and ten; and dark blue, greater than ten. We left the MzLS/BASS region blank because in the paper we have not searched for lenses in that region. Note that observations in <em>grz</em> bands of the northern part (decl.\u00a0&gt;\u00a0\u221230\u00b0) of the Dark Energy Survey (DES; black dotted outline) are included in DECaLS.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf1\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf1\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf1\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>For DECaLS, the delivered image quality has a FWHM of approximately 1.29, 1.18, 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />11 for <em>g</em>, <em>r</em>, and <em>z</em> bands respectively. The MzLS has imaged the <em>\u03b4</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a0+32\u00b0 (NGC) footprint of the Legacy Surveys in <em>z</em>-band that complemented the BASS <em>g</em>- and <em>r</em>-band observations in the same sub-region. While the delivered image quality of MzLS has a median seeing of \u22481<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />01, the median FWHMs for BASS are 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />61 and 1<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />47 in the <em>g</em>- and <em>r</em>-bands, respectively. We choose here to focus on DECaLS due to its better <em>gr</em> seeing than BASS. However, we intend in future work to apply the machine-learning framework we have developed for DECaLS to the northern BASS/MzLS area, by possibly incorporating transfer learning, active learning, or domain adaption (e.g., Tzeng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib55\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib55\">2017</a>) techniques.</p><p>The Legacy Surveys used the Tractor package (Lang et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib32\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib32\">2016</a>) as a forward-modeling approach to perform source extraction on pixel-level data. Tractor takes as input the individual images from multiple exposures in multiple bands, with different seeing in each. After source detection, the point source (\"PSF\") and spatially extended (\"REX,\" round exponential galaxy) models are computed for every source and the better of these two is used when deciding whether to keep the source. The spatially extended sources (REX) are further classified if <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> is improved by 9 by treating it as a deVaucouleurs (DEV), an exponential (EXP) profile, or a composite of DEV + exponential (COMP).<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn2\">13</a></sup>\n The same light profile (EXP, DEV, or COMP) is consistently fit to all images in order to determine the best-fit source shape parameters and photometry.</p><p>The categories of DEV and COMP indicate the classification of elliptical galaxies. Given that the vast majority of lensing events are caused by early-type galaxies, we decided to target only objects with DEV and COMP classifications in this paper.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs3\" name=\"apjab7ffbs3\">3.\u00a0The Training Sample and Residual Neural Networks</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs3-1\" name=\"apjab7ffbs3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Training Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Deep convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and their variations have been shown to be highly effective in image recognition. In recent years, this technique has been successfully applied to recognize instances of strong lenses in simulations (e.g., Metcalf et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib35\">2019</a>, and references therein). In previous applications of CNNs to real observations, training samples are constructed from simulated lens images, combined with observed (Petrillo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib40\">2017</a>), simulated (Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib26\">2017</a>; Pourrahmani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib41\">2018</a>), or a mixture of observed and simulated non-lenses (Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>). This is because the number of known lenses, on the order of several hundred, is thought to be too small to effectively train CNN models. We note that the data set for Jacobs et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib26\">2017</a>) is from the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope (CFHT) Legacy Survey; Petrillo et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib40\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib40\">2017</a>), the Kilo Degree Survey (de Jong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib10\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib10\">2015</a>); Pourrahmani et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib41\">2018</a>), the Hubble Space Telescope ACS <em>i</em>-band observations of the Cosmological Evolution Survey (COSMOS; Capak et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib8\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib8\">2007</a>) field; and Jacobs et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>), the Dark Energy Survey (DES; The Dark Energy Survey Collaboration <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib53\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib53\">2005</a>). All of these searches were performed on completed surveys.</p><p>We decided to use only observed data for lenses and non-lenses in our training sample for partial deployment on DECaLS, which is near completion, and have obtained encouraging results. We identify the known lenses in the Legacy Surveys and DES DR1. A catalog of known lenses in the Legacy Surveys is also necessary in order to identify new lens candidates. Both DECaLS and DES used DECam (see <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib13\">D19</a>). DES has <em>grizY</em> observations with greater depths (see Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a>) in the three bands common with Legacy Surveys. Due to the paucity of lenses, we have used known strong lenses in all of Legacy Surveys, while in this paper we will focus on finding new lenses only in the DECaLS footprint. The Master Lens Database<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn3\">14</a></sup>\n (Moustakas et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib37\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib37\">2012</a>), which contains hundreds of lensing events up to 2016, provided the initial list for the lens training sample. We have since added several hundred more lenses and lens candidates from more recent publications (Carrasco et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib9\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib9\">2017</a>; Diehl et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib14\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib14\">2017</a>; Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib26\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib26\">2017</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>; Pourrahmani et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib41\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib41\">2018</a>; Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib46\">2018</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib60\">2018</a>). In total we have identified ~700 previously known lenses or lens candidates in the Legacy Surveys and DES. A number of these systems were discovered spectroscopically or through imaging with better seeing and/or greater depth than the Legacy Surveys and DES. Some of them therefore have subarcsecond deflection angles and/or lensed sources fainter than can be clearly seen from the DECaLS and DES observations. Through human inspection, we deem 613 as discernible lenses in the Legacy Surveys (199) and DES (414) footprints. For the lenses in the DES footprint, we only include <em>grz</em> bands. We also assemble 13,000 non-lens image cutouts from the Legacy Surveys, all with at least three passes in each of the <em>grz</em> bands. Of these, 5000 are galaxies categorized as DEV or COMP in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib13\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib13\">D19</a> (see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs2\">2</a>), which are elliptical galaxies, and another 5000 of all types of galaxies. For both cases, we apply a <em>z</em>-band magnitude cut of 22.5\u00a0mag. Given that on average we expect one strong lens in <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbieqn2.gif\" alt=\"${ \\mathcal O }({10}^{4})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{ \\mathcal O }({10}^{4})</script></span></span> galaxies (e.g., Oguri &amp; Marshall <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib38\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib38\">2010</a>), incidental inclusion of a lens or two in these randomly selected galaxies is not a significant concern.</p><p>The reason for including non-elliptical galaxies is to provide more non-lens configurations for the neural net. Two of the co-authors have also selected another 3000 non-lenses by eye so as to cover as many non-lens configurations as possible, especially cases that can potentially be confused by the neural net. These include spiral galaxies of different sizes and spiral arm configurations, elliptical galaxies, galaxy groups, images having objects with different colors (typically a blue galaxy next to a red one), cosmic rays appearing in different bands (some of which have curved trajectories), unusual arrangements of galaxies or stars, and finally certain data reduction artefacts (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf2\">2</a>). Simulated non-lenses typically do not cover these scenarios.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf2\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf2\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf2_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 2.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 2.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf2_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 2.</strong>\u00a0Examples of non-lens images in the training sample selected by eye. In order, the seven columns show the categories of (see text): spiral galaxies, ellipitcal galaxies, galaxy groups, galaxies with different colors, cosmic rays, unusual configurations, and finally data reduction artefacts.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf2\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf2\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf2\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure></div><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs3-2\" name=\"apjab7ffbs3-2\">3.2.\u00a0Residual Neural Networks</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We have adopted the Residual Neural Network (ResNet) model of Lanusse et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">2018</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>),<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn4\">15</a></sup>\n which used Theano<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn5\">16</a></sup>\n and Lasagne<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn6\">17</a></sup>\n libraries. We re-implemented their model in TensorFlow,<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn7\">18</a></sup>\n in part because major development for Theano ceased after the 1.0 release on 2017 November 15. We test the translated ResNet model using the simulated training set from the Strong Gravitational Lens Finding Challenge (Metcalf et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib35\">2019</a>) and have reproduced the results in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>, which was the winning entry for the Lens Challenge. The architecture of the model is described in detail in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>.</p><p>\n<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a> has provided much guidance to our approach. At this stage we have left their architecture and hyperparameters unchanged, including the batch size (128), total number of training epochs (120), pre-processing of the images, and data augmentation (random rotation, mirroring, and zooming within a range of [0.9, 1.0]; for details, see Section 3.3 of <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>). The lens and non-lens images in the training sample are cutouts with a dimension of 101\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a0101 pixels, following the specification in the Lens Challenge.</p><p>We split the training sample into training, validation, and testing sets, with ratios of 70:20:10. The sizes of our training, validation, and testing sets are then, respectively, 9876 (423), 2818 (118), and 1427 (72) where the values in parentheses are the number of lenses. We set aside a testing set because we want to leave open the possibility of varying the architecture and hyperparameters to optimize the neural net's performance. We then train the ResNet on the supercomputer Cori<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn8\">19</a></sup>\n at the National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center (NERSC),<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn9\">20</a></sup>\n using three Haswell computing nodes, <sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn10\">21</a></sup>\n one worker each. The 120 epochs of training took 17 hr. The distributed training was accomplished by using Horovod.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn11\">22</a></sup>\n Performing distributed training with deep (46 layers in this case; <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>) neural networks can be non-trivial. We experimented with different numbers of decay epochs and found that with three workers, a decay epoch of 40 (i.e., the learning rate of the ResNet is decreased by a factor 10 every 40 epochs of training) works the best.</p><p>The ResNet attempts to minimize the cross entropy loss function:</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab7ffbeqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbeqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&-\\sum _{i=1}^{N}{y}_{i}\\mathrm{log}{\\hat{y}}_{i}+(1-{y}_{i})\\mathrm{log}(1-{\\hat{y}}_{i})\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>y</em><sub><em>i</em></sub> is label for the <em>i</em>th image (1 for lens and 0 for non-lens), and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbieqn3.gif\" alt=\"${\\hat{y}}_{i}\\in [0,1]$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\hat{y}}_{i}\\in [0,1]</script></span></span> is the model predicted probability.</p><p>While the loss function is monitored during the training process to determine the point of termination, the overall performance of the trained model is typically assessed by the Receiver Operating Characteristic (ROC) curve. The ROC curve shows the True Positive Rate (TPR) versus the False Positive Rate (FPR) for the validation set, where P(ositive) indicates a lens and N(egative), a non-lens. With the definitions TP\u00a0=\u00a0correctly identified as a lens, False Positive\u00a0=\u00a0incorrectly identified as a lens, True Negative\u00a0=\u00a0correctly rejected, and False Negative\u00a0=\u00a0incorrectly rejected,</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab7ffbueqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbueqn1.gif\" alt=\"\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray*}&&\\mathrm{TPR}=\\displaystyle \\frac{\\mathrm{TP}}{{\\rm{P}}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{\\mathrm{TP}}{\\mathrm{TP}+\\mathrm{FN}}\\end{eqnarray*}</script></span></div><p>and</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjab7ffbueqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbueqn2.gif\" alt=\"\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray*}&&\\mathrm{FPR}=\\displaystyle \\frac{\\mathrm{FP}}{{\\rm{N}}}=\\displaystyle \\frac{\\mathrm{FP}}{\\mathrm{FP}+\\mathrm{TN}}.\\end{eqnarray*}</script></span></div><p>The curve is generated by gradually increasing the threshold probability for a positive identification from 0 to 1. Random classifications will result in a diagonal line in this space with an area under the ROC curve (or AUC) equal 0.5. For a perfect classifier, AUC\u00a0=\u00a01.</p><p>The decision of using three nodes was based on our experience with a smaller training set. We can significantly shorten the training time by employing six or more nodes. Since the training set has a total of 9876\u00a0images, with a batch size of 128 images and 3 workers, it takes 26 steps to complete one full training epoch.</p><p>In Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf3\">3</a>, left panel, we show how the cross entropy loss functions vary as training progresses. For the validation set, we show the value at every epoch. For the training set, the cross entropy was reported for every step, which we have boxcar smoothed with a window size of 26. As <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a> also noted, the loss function (Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbeqn1\">1</a>)) and the AUC for the validation set both plateau well within 120 epochs of training. Since the model has performed well, we have left the architecture and hyperparameters in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a> unchanged and moved directly to deployment. Thus, so far we have not used the validation set, or the testing set, for training.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf3\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf3\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf3_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 3.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 3.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf3_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 3.</strong>\u00a0Left: the cross entropy loss functions for the training and validation sets over 120 epochs. Right: the receiver operative characteristic (ROC) curve for the validation set with the area under the curve (AUC)\u00a0=\u00a00.98.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf3\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf3\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf3\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We achieve an AUC of 0.98 for the validation set (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf3\">3</a>, right panel). Even though our training and validation sets contain far fewer lenses, our AUC matches the performance on simulated data in <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a>.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs4\" name=\"apjab7ffbs4\">4.\u00a0Results</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs4-1\" name=\"apjab7ffbs4-1\">4.1.\u00a0Inference and Lens Candidates</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We apply our trained ResNet model to 5.7 million DEV and COMP type galaxies in DECaLS with at least three passes in each of the three bands (<em>grz</em>) and <em>z</em>-band magnitude \u226420.0. This magnitude cut was chosen because it includes 92% of the known lenses in the Legacy Surveys and results in a manageable number of images for human inspection.</p><p>Four co-authors (A. P., C. S., M. D., and V. R.) have inspected ~50,000 cutout images that receive a probability \u22650.9, evenly split. They select candidates according to these criteria, erring on the generous side: small blue galaxy/galaxies (red galaxies are rare but certainly acceptable) next to the red galaxy/galaxies at the center that</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">are typically 1''\u20135'' away</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">have low surface brightness</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">curve toward the red galaxy/galaxies</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">4.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">have counter/multiple images with similar colors (especially in Einstein-cross like configuration)</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">5.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">are elongated (including semi- or nearly full circles).</div></li></ul><p>Typically, most candidates do not have all these characteristics. In general, the greater the number of characteristics listed above an image has, the higher they are ranked by humans. A fifth co-author (X. H.), in consultation with another co-author (R. G.), examines all these \"first pass\" candidates and assigns the grades of A, B, and C, and discards the rest:</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Grade\u00a0A: we have a high level of confidence of these candidates. Many of them have one or more prominent arcs, usually blue. The rest have one or more clear arclets, sometimes arranged in counter-image configurations with similar colors (again, typically blue). However, there are clear cases with red arcs.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Grade\u00a0B: they have similar characteristics as the Grade As. For the cutout images where there appear to be giant arcs they tend to be fainter than those for the Grade As. Likewise, the putative arclets tend to be smaller and/or fainter, or isolated (without counter images).</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">Grade\u00a0C: they generally have features that are even fainter and/or smaller than what is typical for the Grade\u00a0B candidates, but that are nevertheless suggestive of lensed arclets. They are usually without counter images, except for a few cases. In almost all cases, if these are indeed lensing systems, the deflection angles are comparable to or only slightly larger than the seeing.</div></li></ul><p>For Grade\u00a0B and C candidates, we have included a few cases where it is difficult to judge whether it is a lensing event versus a coincidental placement of galaxies, a spiral galaxy, or a ring galaxy. In total we have identified 342 candidates: 60 As, 106 Bs, and 176 Cs, listed in Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbt1\">1</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbt3\">3</a> and shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf4\">4</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf6\">6</a>. The locations of the candidates in the sky are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf7\">7</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a060 Grade A lens candidates arranged in ascending R.A. Each image is 101 pixels \u224826<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 on the side, with N up and E to the left. The exception is DESI-204.0002-03.5250, which is 151 pixel \u224840<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />3 on the side. The seven images with a red rim are later found to be known lenses (see text). These seven lenses were not included in our training sample, and they happen to be in the selected subset of the Legacy Surveys data of 5.7 million elliptical galaxies.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf4\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf4\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf4\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf5\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf5\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf5_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 5.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 5.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf5_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 5.</strong>\u00a0106 Grade B lens candidates. Each image is 101 pixels \u224826<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />2 on the side, with N up and E to the left.
