have spectroscopic redshifts from SDSS (see text). All redshift uncertainties <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$\\lt 3.9\\times {10}^{-4}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\lt 3.9\\times {10}^{-4}</script></span></span>.</p>\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/suppdata/apjab7ffbt3_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/ab7ffb\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a>Typeset images: <a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset-multiple typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/suppdata/apjab7ffbt3_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">1</a> <a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset-multiple typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/suppdata/apjab7ffbt3a_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">2</a> <a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset-multiple typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/suppdata/apjab7ffbt3b_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">3</a> </span></p></div><p>We have checked our candidates against the HST Source Catalog (HSCv3),<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbfn12\">23</a></sup>\n and found six known lenses among the Grade A candidates: DESI-016.3319+01.7490 and DESI-026.2679\u221204.9310 (Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib49\">2013</a>), DESI-036.1436\u221200.0411 (Gladders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib17\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib17\">2003</a>), DESI-168.2943+23.9443 (Kubo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib31\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib31\">2009</a>), DESI-204.0002\u221203.5250 and DESI-219.7907+12.1404 (SDSS DR12 brightest cluster galaxy; Sharon et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib45\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib45\">2020</a>). A seventh, DESI-038.2078-03.3906 (Stark et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib49\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib49\">2013</a>), was pointed out to us during the peer review process (by Chien-Hsiu Lee at NOAO after the paper was posted on the arXiv). These are not in our training sample, and are shown with a red rim in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf4\">4</a>. This leaves the number of new Grade\u00a0A candidates as 53, and the total number of new lens candidates, 335.</p><p>We have found at least 13 new cluster/group scale strong lenses: DESI-019.6618-05.4441, DESI-060.2420-13.9567, DESI-167.8517+14.1473 DESI-219.9855+32.8402, and DESI-359.8897+02.1399 (with a giant red arc) among Grade A, and DESI-009.9772-12.2100, DESI-018.1714-19.0457, DESI-022.3389+00.6547, DESI-023.6765+04.5639, DESI-061.1134-17.2082, DESI-154.7654+17.0697, DESI-202.3729+31.3290, and DESI-216.1003+25.2423 among Grade B candidates.</p><p>Among the hundreds of galaxy scale candidates, there are many notable lensing events. We especially would like to highlight: DESI-135.3125+09.940, a system having a lens with <em>g</em>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>r</em>\u00a0=\u00a03.3, likely indicating a high redshift (e.g., Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>); DESI-041.5205-06.1275, a nearly perfect Einstein cross; and DESI-155.7366-05.0529, with a red arc, which would have a redshift of ~4 if it is a Lyman-break galaxy.</p><p>We would like to emphasize the importance of the Grade B and C candidates. The \"rediscovered lenses\" outside the training sample are in the A category (7 out of 60). This is not a surprise: typically having brighter arcs with larger deflection angles, these systems are comparatively easy to find. Higher redshift lensing systems from ground-based surveys are likely not in the Grade\u00a0A category but in B or C. The current known lensing sample mostly consists of luminous elliptical galaxies at redshifts from approximately 0.4 to 0.8 (e.g., Brownstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib6\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib6\">2012</a>; Wong et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib60\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib60\">2018</a>). Our lens candidates are fainter, and mostly have optical and infrared colors consistent with <em>z</em>\u00a0&gt;\u00a00.8 (e.g., Jacobs et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>). Higher lens redshifts significantly increase the power relative to lower redshift samples for constraining the mass function of low-mass CDM halos, due to the greater optical depth for perturbations by low-mass halos associated with a longer path length along the line of sight (Despali et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib11\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib11\">2018</a>; Ritondale et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib44\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib44\">2019</a>). In addition, the lensed sources will tend to have higher redshifts than in known lensing systems as well.</p><p>Finally, we checked our candidate list against the spectroscopic database from SDSS\u00a0I and II (York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib62\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib62\">2000</a>), SDSS\u00a0III/BOSS (Eisenstein et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib15\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib15\">2011</a>), and SDSS\u00a0IV/eBOSS (Blanton et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib3\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib3\">2017</a>) and found 121 matches, which is slightly greater than one third of all candidates. The available redshifts are included in Tables <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbt1\">1</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbt3\">3</a>.</p><p>The completeness is difficult to estimate at this point, even just for elliptical galaxies because (1) the data reduction for the Legacy Survey has not completed (recall for this deployment, we have only included images with at least three passes in each band), and (2) we have not run inference on the REX category.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs4-2\" name=\"apjab7ffbs4-2\">4.2.\u00a0Probability Bins Lower than 0.9</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>It is notable that there are typically many more candidates with probability greater than 0.9 than with probabilities between 0.8 and 0.9. In a small testing inference run that covers \u22484% of the DECaLS footprint, we have examined and found lens candidates with probability &lt;0.9. The yield typically rapidly diminishes with lower probabilities. As stated earlier, for the deployment on galaxy morphological types DEV and COMP, we impose a magnitude cut at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a020.0 mag. For our small test inference run, we included all objects with <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a022.5 mag. From that run, we have found one Grade\u00a0B lens (DESI-135.9714+07.1954) with a <em>z</em>-band magnitude of 20.87.</p><p>Given that a strong majority of the best lens candidates are from the probability &gt;0.9 bin for the categories of DEV and COMP, in this paper we focus on this subset for human inspection.</p><p>A rough estimate of completeness can be performed by checking how many lensing systems from the validation (118) and testing (72) set would be \"rediscovered.\" This depends on the threshold. 77 of the 190 lenses in the validation and testing sets received a probability of &gt;0.9, or about 40%. The precision (TP/(TP+FP)) versus recall (TP/(TP + FN), or completeness) curve for the validation and testing sets, with probability threshold values marked, are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf8\">8</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf8\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf8\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf8_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 8.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 8.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf8_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 8.</strong>\u00a0Precision-recall curve for the validation and testing sets. The blue points from left to right correspond to probability threshold values from 0.9 to 0.1 with an interval of 0.1.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf8\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf8\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf8\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The implication seems to be that there are hundreds more lenses to be discovered in lower probability bins. We can already confirm there are good lens candidates with probability below 0.9 based on visual inspection for a small subset of the data. However, we caution against a simple forecast based on these percentages. The images in the training sample that receive low probability often are less obvious lenses for the human inspector. In fact for the next round of training, we would remove some of them from the training sample.</p><p>To demonstrate, in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf9\">9</a>, we show four examples of DECaLS images of lensing systems in the current training sample that we would consider for removal. Without prior knowledge, these images could not have been determined to be lenses by visual inspection. The first three are from the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey (SL2S; Cabanac et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib7\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib7\">2007</a>; More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib36\">2012</a>). This survey was carried out on the CFHT in <em>urgiz</em> bands and the lensing systems were found from both their WIDE and DEEP components. They ran semi-automated searches (More et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib36\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib36\">2012</a>) on their <em>g</em>-band images, which are ~1.5\u00a0mag deeper than the 5<em>\u03c3 g</em>-band depth of DECaLS, and has a mean seeing of 0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />78. The DEEP fields were almost 10 times deeper. Therefore some of the blue arcs in the SL2S images are barely visible in their DECaLS counterparts, either due to the shallower <em>g</em>-band depth (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf9\">9</a>, Panels (a) and (b)) and/or the comparatively inferior seeing of DECaLS (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf9\">9</a>, Panel (c)). The fourth example in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf3\">3</a> (Panel (d)) comes from the Hyper Suprime-Cam Subaru Strategic Program (HSC-SSP; Aihara et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib1\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib1\">2018</a>). Observed in <em>grizy</em> bands, this survey also has greater depth and better seeing than DECaLS. As a result, some of the blue arcs seen in the HSC images (Sonnenfeld et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib46\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib46\">2018</a>) are barely visible in DECaLS.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf9\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf9\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf9_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 9.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0Four examples of lensing systems where the blue arcs are very hard to tell, if at all, from DECaLS images, either due to their shallower <em>g</em>-band depth and/or slightly worse seeing (the deflection angle of the system in Panel (d) is ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5). The systems shown in Panels (a)\u2013(c) are from the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey, and the one in (d), the Hyper Suprime-Cam Subaru Strategic Program (see text).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf9\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf9\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>From the experience of this deployment, we believe including images like these in the training sample has resulted in a number of images with high probability that cannot be unambiguously classified by visual inspection. These images significantly decrease the efficiency of human inspection without clear improvement for completeness. This intuition seems to be correct from our preliminary results using an updated training sample. In the future, we will also experiment with getting cutout images directly from the SL2S and HSC surveys.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs4-3\" name=\"apjab7ffbs4-3\">4.3.\u00a0Human Inspection Efficiency</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Below we briefly discuss the human inspection efficiency of the ResNet results thus far. In total we have examined ~50,000 objects. On average, 1 in 150 of the objects with probability from the ResNet model is deemed a lens candidate through human inspection. The Legacy Surveys data is cataloged by Tractor and organized in folders, with each folder corresponding to one degree of R.A. on the sky. The efficiency of our trained ResNet is highly uneven. The number of ResNet-recommended objects per folder in the probability &gt;0.9 bin vary from under 200 to over 3000. We have examined folders with both small and large numbers of objects. In general the folders with lower numbers of objects have higher purity.</p><p>We illustrate this trend in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>, by using the number of passes for <em>z</em>-band as a proxy for depth (as in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a>). The left panel (orange columns) shows that while ~5% of the objects in the 10+ pass bin receive probability &gt;0.9 (\"recommendations\"), only ~0.1% in the 3\u20137 passes bin do. There are certainly more lenses to be found in the deeper regions, but not 50\u00a0times more likely. Human inspection in fact reveals that a much lower percentage of the recommendations from the deepest bin are lens candidates (0.35%), compared with the shallower ones (0.45% for 7\u201310 passes and 1.61% for 3\u20137 passes; Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>, left panel, gray columns). The ResNet's preference for images with greater depth is also shown in the middle panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>: while over 50% of the objects (blue column) are from the shallowest bin (3\u20137 passes), the fraction of recommendations is just below 20% (orange column); in contrast, objects from the deepest bin (10+ passes) only account for ~5% of the objects and yet over 80% of the recommendations are from this bin (again, blue and orange columns, respectively).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0Left: orange columns (left <em>y</em>-axis) show the percentages of objects given a greater than 0.9 probability by our ResNet model (or \"recommendations\") for the three bins of <em>z</em>-band depth. The gray columns (right <em>y</em>-axis) show the percentages of ResNet recommendations that are selected as lens candidates through human inspection. Center: orange columns (left <em>y</em>-axis) show the percentages of ResNet recommendations for each bin of <em>z</em>-band depth. The blue columns (right <em>y</em>-axis) show the percentage of objects in each bin. These two panels show that the ResNet favors images with greater depth whether they contain a lensing system or not. This is a consequence of the composition of our current training sample, as shown in the last panel. Right: yellow (left <em>y</em>-axis) and violet (right <em>y</em>-axis) columns show the percentages of lenses and non-lenses in the training sample, respectively. Overall the images containing lensing systems tend to be much deeper, though a small fraction of them are shallower (see text). It is not a surprise that with such a training sample, we get the distributions of the recommendations and candidates relative to the depth seen in the first two panels.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf10\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf10\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf10\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>This is not a surprise. Since lensed sources typically are faint, most of the lenses in our training sample are in regions of greater depth (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf11\">11</a>, left panel; Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>, right panel, violet columns). The non-lenses in the training sample are mostly selected from shallower regions (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf11\">11</a>, right panel; Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>, right panel, yellow columns). Such a training sample leads the ResNet to assign probabilities not only based on how likely an image contains a lensing system but also its depth. Note that in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf10\">10</a>, Panel\u00a0(c), there is a small fraction of lenses in the training sample with fewer than three passes. Due to the small number of known lenses, we wanted to include as many lenses as possible.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf11\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf11\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf11_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 11.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 11.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf11_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 11.</strong>\u00a0Lenses (red dots, left panel) and non-lenses (black dots, right panel) in our training sample, against the background of the depth map of DECaLS DR7 (see the caption for Figure <a href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a>). The lenses outside the DECaLS footprint are from DES, all with similar depth (10+ passes in <em>z</em> band).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf11\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf11\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf11\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>We have found that the way to address this confusion is to include deep non-lens images. We will perform a full search for DR8, which was completed during the peer review process for this paper, using a larger and more statistically representative training sample (for more details, see Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs5-1\">5.1</a>).</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs5\" name=\"apjab7ffbs5\">5.\u00a0Discussion</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Our results so far are encouraging. In our current training sample we have only used 423 lenses. This is generally considered too small a number for a deep neural net. Nevertheless, we have succeeded in finding hundreds of new lens candidates over a large area of the sky for cutout images centered on elliptical galaxies. Here we will identify where we can improve.</p><p>For DR7, we have inspected the ResNet recommendations for approximately 3/4 of the sky for the DEV and COMP objects. For the remaining 1/4 of the folders, the number of recommendations are all high (<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />1000/folder), which, as mentioned in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs4-3\">4.3</a>, typically translates to low human inspection efficiency. We have stopped human inspection for now.</p><p>Based on our experience so far, we believe we can build a much better training sample, retrain, and deploy for DR8, which, as mentioned earlier, were completed during the referee process for this paper. The coverage and depth map for DR8 are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjab7ffbf12\">12</a>. Note that there are patches where DR7 for DECaLS is deeper than DR8. This is because it is desirable that DR8 has a more uniform coverage.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjab7ffbf12\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjab7ffbf12\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf12_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 12.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 12.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/894/1/78/Full/apjab7ffbf12_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 12.</strong>\u00a0Coverage and depth map for DR8 of the Legacy Surveys. Below the Galactic plane, the footprint extends further south than DR7 (red outline), all of which overlaps with DES (also see Figure <a href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a>). Note that this map includes the MzLS/BASS region. Color scheme for number of passes is the same as in Figure <a href=\"#apjab7ffbf1\">1</a>.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjab7ffbf12\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjab7ffbf12\" href=\"/0004-637X/894/1/78/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjab7ffbf12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs5-1\" name=\"apjab7ffbs5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Toward a Better Training Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We plan to take the following steps to improve our training sample. We need a larger non-lens sample (there are 13,000 in the current training sample) that will cover a greater variety of image configurations and, as mentioned in the previous section, include deep non-lens images. In our experience, having a large number of non-lenses is very helpful in terms of giving the neural net a better chance to reject a diverse variety of non-lenses. This experience comports with what has been reported by other authors. Metcalf et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib35\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib35\">2019</a>) used 20,000 simulated non-lenses in the Lens Competition, which do not include the many complications arising in real observations. Jacobs et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib25\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib25\">2019</a>) used 130,000 non-lenses in their training sample for an ensemble of CNN models to find high-redshift lenses in DES. As stated in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjab7ffbs3\">3</a>, so far we have not used the validation and testing sets in our training. We have ~600 lenses in the entire current training sample. We can add ~160 lens candidates with high confidence, including all Grade\u00a0A and Grade\u00a0B candidates in this paper. Thus we will have ~760 lenses in our next training sample, ~80% more lenses than used for the current trained model.</p><p>In addition, we will make sure to include the following (many of these have been given greater than 0.9 probability of being a lens by the current trained ResNet model): star clusters; instances of interacting galaxies, ring galaxies, and more varieties of spiral galaxies than in the current training sample; and more cosmic-ray examples, especially those in the bluest (<em>g</em>) band and those with curved and/or thick tracks.</p><p>During the peer review process, we have switched our training to graphics processing units and it is clear that the ResNet can train on a much larger sample within a reasonable amount of time. Though at this stage we have left the architecture and hyperparameters of the ResNet from <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">L18</a> unchanged, we may vary both to optimize performance.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs5-2\" name=\"apjab7ffbs5-2\">5.2.\u00a0The MzLS/BASS Region</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The logical next step is to search for lensing systems in the northern MzLS/BASS region for the DEV and COMP categories. In our current training sample, there are 21 lenses from the MzLS/BASS region, which have worse seeings in <em>gr</em> bands. Here we briefly discuss the performance of our current model in this region. Out of a total of the eight lenses in the validation and testing sets (4 each) in MzLS/BASS, a total of three (one in validation and two in testing) have probabilities &gt;0.9, or 37.5%. This is remarkably similar to the 40% completeness for DECaLS. Of course, this is an estimate based on small numbers. Nevertheless it provides a sense of the level of completeness for lenses discoverable in the MzLS/BASS region if we deploy our current trained model. Efforts to adapt our model to this region, to account for the different seeing in <em>gr</em> bands and therefore possibly improve the performance of the ResNet model, is under way.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjab7ffbs5-3\" name=\"apjab7ffbs5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Lenses in the Tractor Galaxy Category of \"REX\"</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The largest number of galaxies, by far, in the Legacy Surveys catalog are classified as the morphological type REX, i.e., the best-fit source model has a round exponential profile. The REX category contains an order of magnitude more objects than the DEV and COMP types combined, since most faint, extended galaxies are modeled by the REX profile. It likely includes many elliptical galaxies, though the percentage is unknown. Even so, given the total number of objects in this category is much larger than those in DEV and COMP categories, there will likely be a large number of lensing systems to be discovered. Among the 199 known lenses from the Legacy Surveys in the training sample, 18 are typed as REX. Out of these, 12 have <em>z</em>\u00a0&lt;\u00a020.0 mag and 1 with <em>z</em>\u00a0= 20.2 mag. We will run inference on this category and report the results in a follow-up publication for lens search in DR8.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjab7ffbs6\" name=\"apjab7ffbs6\">6.\u00a0Conclusions</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have carried out a proof of concept end-to-end implementation of applying a deep residual neural network developed by Lanusse et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjab7ffbbib33\" id=\"fnref-apjab7ffbbib33\">2018</a>), trained on observed lenses and non-lenses, to a subset of the Legacy Surveys data: 5.7 million elliptical galaxies from DECaLS with a <em>z</em>-band magnitude cut of 20.0 mag. We use only real observations for training. In total, we have found 60 Grade A candidates (of these, 53 are new), 106 Grade B, and 176 Grade C candidates (all new).</p><p>The results are promising. Despite using a relative small training set with 423 lens and 9451 non-lenses for a survey with non-uniform depth (given that the survey was not yet completed), in this paper we report the discovery of the first batch of 335 new strong lens candidates from the Legacy Surveys. We will improve our training sample and model for the next round of training for full deployment on the entire Legacy Surveys DR8 footprint.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We thank Steve Farrell, Mustafa Mustafa, Laurie Stephey, Rollin Thomas, and Prabat at the National Energy Scientific Computating Center (NERSC) for their consultation and advice. We thank Greg Aldering for insightful conversations in compiling our training sample. We thank Andi Gu for providing assistance for rendering some of the figures. We are grateful to Joel Brownstein and Lexi Moustakas for granting us access to the Master Lens Database (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"webref\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"http://admin.masterlens.org/index.php\">http://admin.masterlens.org/index.php</a>). This research used resources of the National Energy Research Scientific Computing Center (NERSC), a U.S. Department of Energy Office of Science User Facility operated under Contract No. DE-AC02-05CH11231 and the Computational HEP program in The Department of Energy's Science Office of High Energy Physics provided resources through the \"Cosmology Data Repository\" project (Grant #KA2401022). X.H. acknowledges the University of San Francisco Faculty Development Fund. A.D.'s research is supported by the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy (AURA) under cooperative agreement with the National Science Foundation.</p><p>This paper is based on observations at Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory, National Optical Astronomy Observatory (NOAO Prop. ID: 2014B-0404; co-PIs: D. J. Schlegel and A. Dey), which is operated by the Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy (AURA) under a cooperative agreement with the National Science Foundation.</p><p>This project used data obtained with the Dark Energy Camera (DECam), which was constructed by the Dark Energy Survey (DES) collaboration. Funding for the DES Projects has been provided by the U.S. Department of Energy, the U.S. National Science Foundation, the Ministry of Science and Education of Spain, the Science and Technology Facilities Council of the United Kingdom, the Higher Education Funding Council for England, the National Center for Supercomputing Applications at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Kavli Institute of Cosmological Physics at the University of Chicago, the Center for Cosmology and Astro-Particle Physics at the Ohio State University, the Mitchell Institute for Fundamental Physics and Astronomy at Texas A&amp;M University, Financiadora de Estudos e Projetos, Funda\u00e7\u00e3o Carlos Chagas Filho de Amparo \u00e0 Pesquisa do Estado do Rio de Janeiro, Conselho Nacional de Desenvolvimento Cient\u00edfico e Tecnol\u00f3gico and the Minist\u00e9rio da Ci\u00eancia, Tecnologia e Inovac\u00e3o, the Deutsche Forschungsgemeinschaft, and the Collaborating Institutions in the Dark Energy Survey. The Collaborating Institutions are Argonne National Laboratory, the University of California at Santa Cruz, the University of Cambridge, Centro de Investigaciones En\u00e9rgeticas, Medioambientales y Tecnol\u00f3gicas-Madrid, the University of Chicago, University College London, the DES-Brazil Consortium, the University of Edinburgh, the Eidgen\u00f6ssische Technische Hochschule (ETH) Z\u00fcrich, Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory, the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, the Institut de Ci\u00e8ncies de l'Espai (IEEC/CSIC), the Institut de F\u00edsica d'Altes Energies, Lawrence Berkeley National Laboratory, the Ludwig-Maximilians Universit\u00e4t M\u00fcnchen and the associated Excellence Cluster Universe, the University of Michigan, the National Optical Astronomy Observatory, the University of Nottingham, the Ohio State University, the OzDES Membership Consortium the University of Pennsylvania, the University of Portsmouth, SLAC National Accelerator Laboratory, Stanford University, the University of Sussex, and Texas A&amp;M University.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 The combination of the depth and breadth of these surveys are unparalleled at this time, making them particularly suitable for discovering new strong gravitational lensing systems. We adopt the deep residual neural network architecture developed by Lanusse et al. for the purpose of finding strong lenses in photometric surveys. We compile a training sample that consists of known lensing systems in the Legacy Surveys and the Dark Energy Survey as well as non-lenses in the footprint of DECaLS. In this paper we show the results of applying our trained neural network to the cutout images centered on galaxies typed as ellipticals in DECaLS. The images that receive the highest scores (probabilities) are visually inspected and ranked.
